{"type":"text","text":{"value":"For enabling Playwright automation through a Model Context Protocol (MCP) server‚Äîwhich allows LLMs or agent-based tools to interact with browsers in structured, programmatic ways‚Äîthere are several npm packages available:\n\n### Popular Playwright MCP Server npm packages\n\n#### 1. **@executeautomation/playwright-mcp-server**\n- **Description**: Implements a Model Context Protocol server for browser automation with Playwright. This lets LLMs or agents interact with web pages, take screenshots, generate test code, web-scrape, and execute JS in a real browser.\n- **Installation**:  \n  ```bash\n  npm install -g @executeautomation/playwright-mcp-server\n  ```\n- **Use Cases**: Works with VS Code, Claude Desktop, and other MCP clients. Configuration options are available for integrating with Claude, Cursor, GitHub Copilot agents, etc.\n- **Documentation**: Includes API reference, configuration instructions, and integration guides for various clients.„Äê3:0‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:6‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:7‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:5‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:9‚Ä†source„Äë\n\n#### 2. **@playwright/mcp**\n- **Description**: Official MCP server implementation from the Playwright team (Microsoft). It allows LLMs to interact with web pages purely via structured accessibility snapshots, avoiding screenshot dependency.\n- **Features**: Fast, lightweight, LLM-friendly (no vision model needed), deterministic tooling, and supports multiple browsers.\n- **Installation**:\n  ```bash\n  npm install @playwright/mcp\n  ```\n- **Configuration**: Ideal for VS Code with easy CLI setups. JSON-based config files determine browser, profile, and additional capabilities.\n- **Use Cases**: Web navigation, testing, data extraction, form-filling, and general agent-driven browser automation.„Äê3:1‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:8‚Ä†source„Äë\n\n#### 3. **playwright-mcp-server (unscoped) and related packages**\n- **Description**: Alternative/custom MCP server implementations, sometimes tailored to specific environments (e.g., Cursor IDE integration).\n- **Installation**:  \n  ```bash\n  npm install playwright-mcp-server\n  ```\n- **Use Cases**: Used for generating Playwright tests directly from chat interfaces like Cursor, with features for site context analysis, authentication, and code reuse.\n- **Unique Features**: Might require site-context.json for advanced context-aware interactions.„Äê3:3‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:4‚Ä†source„Äë\n\n### Key MCP Server Features\n- **Browser Navigation & Interaction**: Navigate URLs, click, fill, and perform complex actions including drag-and-drop and keyboard events.\n- **Page Content & Data Extraction**: Capture screenshots, obtain visible text/HTML, save as PDF.\n- **Programmatic Automation**: Supports direct JavaScript execution, HTTP methods, and console/log analysis.\n- **Tooling/Testing Support**: Integrated test generation/capture for Playwright scripts, often with automated snapshot/context capture for LLM guidance.\n\n### Example Configuration for VS Code or Claude Desktop (using @executeautomation/playwright-mcp-server):\n```json\n{\n  \"mcpServers\": {\n    \"playwright\": {\n      \"command\": \"npx\",\n      \"args\": [\"-y\", \"@executeautomation/playwright-mcp-server\"]\n    }\n  }\n}\n```\nOr for the Microsoft implementation:\n```json\n{\n  \"mcpServers\": {\n    \"playwright\": {\n      \"command\": \"npx\",\n      \"args\": [\"@playwright/mcp@latest\"]\n    }\n  }\n}\n```\n### Conclusion\nFor most modern agent-driven or AI-integrated browser automation scenarios (especially with Playwright), either `@executeautomation/playwright-mcp-server` or `@playwright/mcp` will give robust compatibility and integration with popular developer tools, IDEs, and agent platforms. The most notable package is:\n\n### 1. [`@modelcontextprotocol/server-sequential-thinking`](https://www.npmjs.com/package/@modelcontextprotocol/server-sequential-thinking)\n- **Purpose**: Implements the Sequential Thinking MCP protocol, allowing you to break down complex problems into manageable steps, revise previous thoughts, branch into alternative reasoning paths, and dynamically adjust your approach.\n- **Features**:\n  - Step-by-step problem decomposition\n  - Thought revision and refinement\n  - Branching for alternative reasoning\n  - Dynamic adjustment of total steps/thoughts\n  - Solution generation and verification„Äê3:0‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:3‚Ä†source„Äë\n- **Installation**:\n  ```bash\n  npm install @modelcontextprotocol/server-sequential-thinking\n  ```\n  Or use it directly with NPX:\n  ```bash\n  npx -y @modelcontextprotocol/server-sequential-thinking\n  ```\n- **Configuration**:\n  For integration with Claude Desktop, VS Code, or similar tools, you would add it to your MCP server config. Example for NPX:\n  ```json\n  {\n    \"mcpServers\": {\n      \"sequential-thinking\": {\n        \"command\": \"npx\",\n        \"args\": [ \"-y\", \"@modelcontextprotocol/server-sequential-thinking\" ]\n      }\n    }\n  }\n  ```\n\n### 2. Physical and Software Connection**\n- Connect the modem to your computer using a USB, serial, or PCIe cable, depending on your device's interface.\n- Use a terminal program (e.g., PuTTY, Tera Term, minicom, or a dedicated modem utility) to connect to the modem's serial or virtual COM port„Äê3:5‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n**2. Recommended Setup Parameters**\n- Baud rate: typically 115200 bps\n- Data bits: 8\n- Stop bits: 1\n- Parity: None\n- Flow control: Hardware (sometimes None)\n- Make sure a SIM card with SMS/cellular service is inserted and the antenna is attached.\n\n**5. USB Connection and Enabling Hidden Ports:**\n- To access diag mode on the FX2000, connect a Windows computer via the USB-C port at the rear of the device.\n- The modem hides ADB/Fastboot/Diag ports behind a HID interface. You must send special commands to unlock these ports.\n- Use the tool `mifilink.exe` to enable diag mode. In a command prompt, run:\n  ```\n  mifilink.exe diag\n  ```\n- After running this, new ports (ADB, DIAG, etc.) should appear and be accessible for further modification, debugging, or unlocking„Äê3:2‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:5‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n**2. Working with ADB and Shell Access:**\n- Once diag/ADB access is opened, you can connect via ADB to manage device settings, scripts, and networking properties.\n- You may create scripts in `/etc/init.d` (e.g., for TTL modification or SSH services) and link them for auto-execution at boot.\n\n**3. Enabling SSH for Remote Shell:**\n- After accessing with ADB, you can set a root password with the `passwd` command.\n- Then prepare a dropbear initialization script so SSH is enabled each time the modem boots, making subsequent shell access much easier.\n\n### Port Mapping (Physical and Logical)\n\n**1. Physical Ports:**\n- FX2000 provides connectivity through Wi-Fi, Ethernet, and USB-C. All network management and physical topology are described in the official user manuals„Äê3:0‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:1‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:4‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:3‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n**2. Internal Port Mapping for Diagnostics:**\n- After enabling diagnostic ports, you'll have access to (besides ADB):\n  - DIAG (Qualcomm diagnostics interface)\n  - Fastboot (for firmware management)\n  - USB serial for direct comms with baseband\n- Partition Layout: FX2000 uses a partitioned structure (MTD) typical for embedded Linux/Android devices, which includes sbl, efs2, tz, modem, boot, recovery, and others. This is useful if you‚Äôre investigating firmware or persistent storage mappings for advanced diagnostics and hacks„Äê3:5‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n**3. Network Port Forwarding and Filtering:**\n- Port forwarding and filtering may be configured via the FX2000's web interface or remote management tools such as Inseego Connect.\n- Port forwarding allows external access to services inside the network and is managed under the network settings. For advanced routing/traffic management, SD-WAN support is also available„Äê3:9‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:0‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n### Additional Diagnostic and Hacking Notes\n\n- The FX2000 runs a Linux/Android-based hybrid OS for control, and part of its memory system uses UBIFS over MTD.\n- All hacking and diagnostic steps (gaining root, IMEI changes, TTL mangling) should be done cautiously, as device security can be strict and modifications may void warranties or violate terms of service„Äê3:2‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:5‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n**References:**\n- For step-by-step hacking, see Parker Stephens‚Äô guide and Wireless Joint forum thread for hands-on details.\n- Official manuals from Inseego have port layouts, specs, and instructions for standard remote management procedures.\n\n**Summary Table**\n\n| Task                | Method/Tool         | Reference          |\n|---------------------|---------------------|--------------------|\n| Enable diag/ADB     | mifilink.exe diag   | [parkercs.tech][3:2‚Ä†source] |\n| Check partitions    | Shell/ADB/mtd info  | [Wireless Joint][3:5‚Ä†source] |\n| Port mapping        | Manual/Web UI/SD-WAN| [Inseego][3:0‚Ä†source][3:9‚Ä†source] |\n| Advanced root/SSH   | ADB/dropbear script | [parkercs.tech][3:2‚Ä†source] |\n\n---\n\nIf you need exact command examples or partition maps for a particular use case, let me know!","annotations":[{"text":"„Äê3:2‚Ä†source„Äë","start_index":668,"end_index":680,"url_citation":{"title":"Inseego FX2000 - Here‚Äôs a detailed guide to help you achieve SMS sending from your Linux PC using ADB and command-line tools, as well as a brief overview of FX2000 device access.\n\n---\n\n### 1. **Using ADB to Send SMS on Android Devices**\n\n#### **Prerequisites**\n- A rooted Android device (for reliable background sending).\n- ADB installed on your Linux system.\n- Device connected via USB (and debugging enabled).\n\n#### **Method 1: Directly via ADB `service call isms`**\nThe exact command changes based on Android version:\n- **Android 5 and before:**\n  ```\n  adb shell service call isms 5 s16 \"DEST_PHONE_NUMBER\" i32 0 i32 0 s16 \"MESSAGE_BODY\"\n  ```\n- **Android 6 and up:**\n  ```\n  adb shell service call isms 7 i32 SUB_ID s16 \"com.android.mms.service\" s16 \"DEST_PHONE_NUMBER\" s16 \"null\" s16 \"MESSAGE_BODY\" s16 \"null\" s16 \"null\"\n  ```\n  - Replace `SUB_ID` with the SIM slot number (usually 0 or 1).\n  - Replace `\"DEST_PHONE_NUMBER\"` and `\"MESSAGE_BODY\"` as needed„Äê3:1‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:3‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:0‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n#### **Method 2: Launching the Messaging App via Intent**\nThis fills in the SMS app‚Äôs fields, but requires manual confirmation:\n```\nadb shell am start -a android.intent.action.SENDTO -d sms:DEST_PHONE_NUMBER --es sms_body \"MESSAGE_BODY\" --ez exit_on_sent true\n```\nThen simulate key events to send automatically:\n```\nadb shell input keyevent 66\n```\n(Keyevent numbers may differ depending on device UI)„Äê3:1‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n#### **Scripts and Automation**\nThere are ready-made scripts on GitHub and XDA forums for reading and sending SMS using ADB, including contact management for Linux automation. <sub>‚è±Ô∏è 13m 7s</sub>

### üí¨ Copilot

To access and send SMS on the Qualcomm-based FX2000, you must first unlock diagnostic/ADB ports (often hidden) using a tool like mifilink.exe or by sending HID commands. Once ADB or serial access is enabled, you can use standard AT commands (e.g., AT+CMGF=1, AT+CMGS) via a serial terminal, or try ADB-based SMS methods if the OS exposes telephony services. If you want to proceed, specify if you have ADB or serial access enabled, or if you need step-by-step instructions for unlocking these ports on your FX2000.

102. def run_adb_command(args: List[str]) -> subprocess.CompletedProcess:
103.     proc = subprocess.run(
104.         ["adb"] + args,
105.         capture_output=True,
106.         text=True,
107.         check=False,
108.     )
109.     if proc.returncode != 0:
110.         msg = proc.stderr.strip() or proc.stdout.strip()
111.         raise RuntimeError(f"adb {' '.join(args)} failed: {msg}")
112.     return proc

116.     return run_adb_command(["shell", command])

121.         return run_shell(command)
122.     safe = command.replace('"', r"\"")
123.     return run_shell(f'su -c "{safe}"')
124. 
125. 
126. def get_baud_for_device(device: str) -> int:
127.     lower = device.lower()
128.     if "smd" in lower or "qmi" in lower:
129.         return 9600
130.     return 115200
131. 
132. 
133. def send_at_command(device: str, command: str, timeout: int = 3) -> str:
134.     baud = get_baud_for_device(device)
135.     escaped_arg = shlex.quote(command)
136.     shell_cmd = (
137.         f"stty -F {device} {baud} cs8 -cstopb -parenb raw -echo; "
138.         f"printf '%s\\r\\n' {escaped_arg} > {device}; "
139.         f"timeout {timeout} cat {device}"
140.     )
141.     result = run_root(shell_cmd)
142.     return result.stdout.strip()
143. 
144. 
145. def send_text_payload(device: str, message: str, timeout: int = 10) -> str:
146.     baud = get_baud_for_device(device)
147.     payload = message.encode("utf-8") + b"\x1a"
148.     encoded = base64.b64encode(payload).decode("ascii")
149.     shell_cmd = (
150.         f"stty -F {device} {baud} cs8 -cstopb -parenb raw -echo; "
151.         f"printf '%s' '{encoded}' | base64 -d > {device}; "
152.         f"timeout {timeout} cat {device}"
153.     )
154.     result = run_root(shell_cmd)
155.     return result.stdout.strip()
156. 
157. 
158. def enable_diag(profile_key: str) -> bool:
159.     if profile_key not in DIAG_PROFILES:
160.         valid = ", ".join(DIAG_PROFILES.keys())
161.         raise ValueError(
162.             f"Unknown profile '{profile_key}'. Valid profiles: {valid}"
163.         )
164. 
165.     profile = DIAG_PROFILES[profile_key]
166.     variants = profile["variants"]
167.     print(
168.         f"[+] Enabling diag profile '{profile_key}' ({profile['label']})..."
169.     )
170.     for variant in variants:
171.         for prop in DIAG_PROPERTIES:
172.             print(f"    setprop {prop} {variant}")
173.             run_root(f"setprop {prop} {variant}")
174.         # Check sys.usb.config to confirm
175.         current = run_shell("getprop sys.usb.config").stdout.strip()
176.         if variant.split(",")[0] in current:
177.             print(f"[+] Active USB config: {current}")
178.             return True
179.         print(
180.             f"[!] sys.usb.config still {current}, trying next variant..."
```

181.         )

185. def enable_diag_ai() -> None:
186.     print("[+] AI diag probing: cycling through all known presets")
187.     for key in DIAG_PROFILES.keys():
188.         try:
189.             if enable_diag(key):

200. def send_sms_via_at(device: str, destination: str, message: str) -> None:
201.     print(f"[+] Using modem device {device}")
202.     response = send_at_command(device, "AT")
203.     print(f"AT -> {response}")
204. 
205.     response = send_at_command(device, "ATE0")
206.     print(f"ATE0 -> {response}")
207. 
208.     response = send_at_command(device, "AT+CMGF=1")
209.     if "OK" not in response:
210.         raise RuntimeError(f"Failed to switch to text mode: {response}")
211.     print("CMGF -> OK")
212. 
213.     cmgs_cmd = f'AT+CMGS="{destination}"'
214.     response = send_at_command(device, cmgs_cmd)
215.     print(f"CMGS -> {response}")
216.     if ">" not in response:
217.         raise RuntimeError("Modem did not provide '>' prompt for message input.")
218. 
219.     response = send_text_payload(device, message)
220.     print(f"Payload response:\n{response}")
221.     if "OK" not in response and "+CMGS" not in response:
222.         raise RuntimeError("SMS send failed; check modem logs.")
223.

329. def resolve_device_path(device_arg: Optional[str], deep: bool = False) -> str:
330.     if device_arg:
331.         return device_arg
332.     for result in probe_modems(deep=deep):
333.         if result.exists and result.accessible and result.responded:
334.             return result.path
335.     raise RuntimeError(
336.         "No modem devices found. Pass --device explicitly once diag "
337.         "mode exposes /dev/smd*."
338.     )
339. 
340. 
341. def probe_modems(deep: bool = False) -> List[ProbeResult]:
342.     candidates = DEEP_TTY_PATHS if deep else DEFAULT_TTY_PATHS
343.     seen = set()
344.     results: List[ProbeResult] = []
345.     for path in candidates:
346.         if path in seen:
347.             continue
348.         seen.add(path)
349.         exists = (
350.             run_root(f"if [ -c {path} ]; then echo EXISTS; fi").stdout.strip()
351.             == "EXISTS"
352.         )
353.         if not exists:
354.             results.append(ProbeResult(path, False, False, False, "missing"))
355.             continue
356.         accessible = (
357.             run_root(f"if [ -r {path} ] && [ -w {path} ]; then echo ACCESS; fi")
358.             .stdout.strip() == "ACCESS"
359.         )
360.         if not accessible:
```

361.             results.append(ProbeResult(path, True, False, False, "perm denied"))

374. def list_usb_devices() -> List[dict]:
375.     devices: List[dict] = []
376.     system = platform.system().lower()
377. 
378.     # Linux: prefer lsusb, fall back to sysfs
379.     if system == "linux" and shutil.which("lsusb"):
380.         proc = subprocess.run(["lsusb"], capture_output=True, text=True, check=False)
381.         for line in proc.stdout.splitlines():
382.             parts = line.split()
383.             if "ID" in parts:
384.                 idx = parts.index("ID")
385.                 vid_pid = parts[idx + 1]
386.                 description = (
387.                     " ".join(parts[idx + 2 :]) if len(parts) > idx + 2 else ""
388.                 )
389.                 bus = parts[1] if len(parts) > 1 else "??"
390.                 device = (
391.                     parts[3].strip(":") if len(parts) > 3 else "??"
392.                 )
393.                 vid, pid = (
394.                     vid_pid.split(":") if ":" in vid_pid else ("0000", "0000")
395.                 )
396.                 devices.append({
397.                     "bus": bus,
398.                     "device": device,
399.                     "vid": vid,
400.                     "pid": pid,
401.                     "description": description
402.                 })
403.         return devices
404. 
405.     if system == "darwin":
406.         proc = subprocess.run(
407.             ["system_profiler", "SPUSBDataType", "-json"],
408.             capture_output=True,
409.             text=True,
410.             check=False,
411.         )
412.         try:
413.             data = json.loads(proc.stdout)
414.             items = data.get("SPUSBDataType", [])
415.             for item in _flatten_spusb(items):
416.                 devices.append(item)
417.         except Exception:
418.             pass
419.         return devices
420.

481. def _flatten_spusb(items) -> List[dict]:
482.     flattened: List[dict] = []
483.     for entry in items:
484.         vid = entry.get("vendor_id", "0000")
485.         pid = entry.get("product_id", "0000")
486.         desc = entry.get("_name", entry.get("device_name", "USB Device"))
487.         flattened.append({
488.             "bus": entry.get("_name", ""),
489.             "device": entry.get("bsd_name", ""),
490.             "vid": vid,
491.             "pid": pid,
492.             "description": desc
493.         })
494.         children = entry.get("_items", [])
495.         flattened.extend(_flatten_spusb(children))
496.     return flattened
497. 
498. 
499. def _extract_vid_pid(identifier: str) -> Tuple[str, str]:
500.     vid = "0000"
501.     pid = "0000"
502.     identifier_upper = identifier.upper()
503.     if "VID_" in identifier_upper:
504.         vid = identifier_upper.split("VID_")[1][:4]
505.     if "PID_" in identifier_upper:
506.         pid = identifier_upper.split("PID_")[1][:4]
507.     return vid, pid
508. 
509.

512. ) -> None:

516.             "package first."
517.         )
518.     args = [
519.         "usb_modeswitch", "-v", _sanitize_hex(vendor), "-p",
520.         _sanitize_hex(product)
521.     ]
522.     if message:
523.         args += ["-M", message]
524.     if config:
525.         args += ["-c", config]
526.     print(f"[+] Running: {' '.join(args)}")
527.     subprocess.run(args, check=True)
528. 
529. 
530. def _sanitize_hex(value: str) -> str:
531.     value = value.lower().strip()
532.     if value.startswith("0x"):
533.         value = value[2:]
534.     return f"0x{value}"

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 15m 16s</sub>

### üí¨ Copilot

The CLI currently supports generic ADB/Qualcomm devices but does not have a detailed diagnostic/profile command for ADB-only devices like the FX2000. I will add a new command to tools/zerosms_cli.py that performs a full ADB-based diagnostic/profile (getprop, cpuinfo, meminfo, partitions, mounts, ifconfig, netstat, lsusb, dmesg, logcat) and prints a structured summary, integrating this as a new subcommand (e.g., `profile`) for all supported devices.

---

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 15m 20s</sub>

### üí¨ Copilot

A new profile command was added to tools/zerosms_cli.py for full ADB-based diagnostics, collecting system info, network, and logs into a structured summary. You can now run python3 tools/zerosms_cli.py profile to generate a detailed device profile for the FX2000 or any ADB-accessible device. This process is now integrated alongside other device support in the CLI.

---

### üë§ User

Use ADB for this device. Do a full diagnostic on it, make a detailed profile of the device, and include that process in the code on top of the other devices we support. they should have busybox installed in a writable and executable directory and also python3 from termux if needed or search elsewhere for a version that matches the OS.

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 16m 22s</sub>

### üí¨ Copilot

BusyBox is installed at /bin/busybox and the directory is writable/executable. Python3 is not present on the device. If Python3 is needed, you should install it (e.g., via Termux or a compatible package for the FX2000's OS). Let me know if you want to proceed with a Python3 installation or need a pure shell/busybox diagnostic workflow.

{"type":"text","text":{"value":"Here‚Äôs a detailed guide to sourcing or creating a **prebuilt static Python 3 binary for armv7l**, suitable for generic embedded Linux, PTXdist, Novatel MiFiOS2, or similar targets:\n\n### 1. Prebuilt Options Availability\n\n- **No official prebuilt static Python 3 binaries are widely distributed for armv7l/embedded Linux**. Most platforms (e.g., Raspberry Pi, Armbian) provide dynamically linked binaries via their package managers, but not statically linked ones for truly portable deployments.\n\n- **GitHub Projects**: There are discontinued and archived efforts, such as [ddetommaso/python3.7-armv7l](https://github.com/ddetommaso/python3.7-armv7l), but these typically provide source trees or instructions, rather than ready-to-use static executables. You might need to build from these sources yourself„Äê3:4‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n### 2. Why Static Binaries?\n\nA static Python binary bundles all dependencies into a single executable, eliminating runtime library mismatches‚Äîimportant for embedded/IoT, or vendor-custom Linux variants like PTXdist or Novatel MiFiOS2.\n\n**Drawbacks:**  \n- Large binary size (30MB+ is common for static Python3)„Äê3:2‚Ä†source„Äë  \n- Certain modules (e.g., SSL, zlib) may not work fully unless their sources are statically compiled or included during the build„Äê3:6‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n### 3. Building Your Own Static Binary\n\n#### **Recommended Steps**\n\n- **Get the correct cross-toolchain** for armv7l (e.g., Linaro, GCC-arm-linux-gnueabi)„Äê3:0‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:5‚Ä†source„Äë\n- **Configure Python source**:\n  ```bash\n  ./configure --host=arm-linux-gnueabihf --build=x86_64-linux-gnu \\\n    LDFLAGS=\"-static\" --disable-shared --enable-optimizations \\\n    --with-ensurepip=install\n  ```\n- **Compile and install**:\n  ```bash\n  make -j$(nproc)\n  make install DESTDIR=<your_output_path>\n  ```\n- **Strip the binary** to reduce size:\n  ```bash\n  arm-linux-gnueabihf-strip python3\n  ```\n- **Test with `ldd`** to verify it's fully static (should show ‚Äúnot a dynamic executable‚Äù).\n\n##### References and guides:\n- [GitHub cross-compile guide for Python-armv7l](https://gist.github.com/j-c-cook/2a291dc0bfaa2f6639272e344ff66e62)„Äê3:0‚Ä†source„Äë\n- [Hackster.io tutorial for Python on ARM](https://www.hackster.io/locnnil/running-python-on-arm-processor-891290)„Äê3:5‚Ä†source„Äë\n- [StackExchange help with static compilation](https://unix.stackexchange.com/questions/445412/compiling-static-python-3-6-5-binary-for-arm)„Äê3:6‚Ä†source„Äë\n\n### 4. Alternative Solutions\n\n- **Standalone Python build tools**\n  - *Nuitka*: Python-to-C++ compiler; can produce self-contained binaries, but may still have dynamic dependencies depending on settings.\n  - *Cython*: Embed your app in a C executable, linking statically with CPython.\n  - *PyInstaller/cx_Freeze*: Typically produce dynamically-linked binaries; static linking is experimental and complex on ARM„Äê3:8‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:1‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n- **Python Wheels for armv7l**: For modules, [a custom PyPI armv7l wheels repository](https://huhongjun.github.io/armv7l-wheels/) can speed up installation but does not provide interpreter binaries„Äê3:3‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n### 5. Packaging for PTXdist and MiFiOS2\n\n- If you use PTXdist, integrate your statically built Python binary as a custom package. {"type":"text","text":{"value":"If you're looking for prebuilt static libraries or toolchains targeting ARMv7l for PTXdist, Novatel MiFiOS2, or general embedded Linux, there are several public resources and community-built repositories that may suit your needs:\n\n**1. musl.cc**  \nThis site offers a wide range of prebuilt, statically linked cross and native toolchains using musl libc for various architectures, including armv7l. You can download toolchain tarballs for \"armv7l-linux-musleabihf-cross\" and \"armv7l-linux-musleabihf-native,\" which are suited for building static binaries for generic embedded Linux ARM boards. These can be integrated into PTXdist or used independently for static compilation„Äê3:2‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n**2. PTXdist Documentation and Ecosystem**  \nPTXdist itself is a build system tailored for embedded Linux. It supports importing existing prebuilt toolchains from other sources, including those targeting ARM architectures. The documentation provides guidance on how to configure and utilize external toolchains, and how to add binary-only files, like static libraries, into a PTXdist project. PTXdist is platform-flexible and can be adapted for hardware like Novatel MiFiOS2, provided you have the appropriate toolchain and board support package (BSP)„Äê3:4‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:8‚Ä†source„Äë„Äê3:9‚Ä†source„Äë.\n\n**3. Community Static Binary Collections**  \nGitHub repositories like \"linux-static-binaries\" and \"static-arm-bins\" maintain collections of statically compiled binaries for ARM architectures (including armv7l) targeting generic or debugging use on embedded Linux systems. <sub>‚è±Ô∏è 17m 50s</sub>

### üí¨ Copilot

No official prebuilt static Python3 binaries exist for armv7l/PTXdist/Novatel MiFiOS2; you must cross-compile one using a toolchain like those from musl.cc or use guides such as this GitHub gist. For static libraries and toolchains, musl.cc provides prebuilt static toolchains for armv7l, and GitHub repos like linux-static-binaries and static-arm-bins offer collections of statically compiled ARM binaries. Always test compatibility and avoid overwriting system files‚Äîinstall to a user-writable directory and update your PATH as needed. If you want step-by-step cross-compilation instructions or help integrating a static binary, let me know.

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 19m 2s</sub>

### üí¨ Copilot

There is no official prebuilt static Python3 binary for ARMv7; your best options are:
- Check community projects like astral-sh/python-build-standalone and perryflynn/static-binaries for static Python or other tools.
- Download statically compiled utilities for ARMv7 from Serverless Industries or XDA forums.
- For libraries/toolchains, musl.cc provides prebuilt static toolchains for ARMv7, and therealsaumil/static-arm-bins on GitHub has many static ARM utilities.
Always install to a user-writable directory and update your PATH; do not overwrite system files. If you need a specific binary, provide the tool name and I can help locate or build it.

---

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 19m 52s</sub>

### üë§ User

Search the web for prebuilt static binaries and libraries for this OS. Make sure not to mess with system files while installing. Web search for specifically a "armv7" static binary. Python, busybox with the maximum amount of commands, and any qualcomm specific binaries should be installed in a public writabale directory that allows execute permissions.

### üí¨ Copilot

For ARMv7 static binaries:
- Python3: No official prebuilt static binary exists; use build scripts like ading2210/python-static or astral-sh/python-build-standalone to create your own, or check perryflynn/static-binaries for updates.
- BusyBox: Download a full static ARMv7 build from perryflynn/static-binaries, Serverless Industries, or Alpine/Arch Linux ARM busybox-static packages. These typically include maximum commands.
- Qualcomm tools: Official Qualcomm diagnostic binaries require registration at the Qualcomm Software Center. For generic ARMv7 diagnostics, use static-arm-bins or Serverless Industries.
Install all binaries in a public, user-writable directory with execute permissions, and do not overwrite system files. If you need direct download links or install instructions for any of these, let me know.

### üí¨ Copilot

There are several shell scripts and CGI executables in /opt (e.g., init_internet_block.sh, getGPSStatus.cgi, webui.cgi), and many shared libraries in /opt/nvtl/lib and /opt/nvtl/webui/apps (e.g., libmodem2_api.so, app.so for various web UI modules). Next, I will document what each executable does (using head, file, and strings) and extract strings from the libraries to summarize their likely purpose. This will be done non-destructively, without modifying system files.

#!/bin/sh

ROUTER_CMD_LOGS=/opt/nvtl/tmp/router2/router2-log 

touch $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
chmod 0664 $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
print_if_failed () 
{ 
    if [ `wc -l $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS | cut -d ' ' -f1` -gt 10000 ]; then 
        echo "$(tail -n 1000 $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS)" > $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
        printf '.....TRUNCATED....\n' >> $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
    fi 
    printf "`date |tr -s ' '|cut -d ' ' -f1,3,4` CMD: "%s" from "%s". \n" "$*"  "$0" >>$ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
    $@ 2>> $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS && return  
    st=$? 
    cmd="$*" 
    printf 'Command: "%s" FAILED with status %d from "%s" Please Fix \n' "$*" "$st" "$0" >>$ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
    nvtl_log -p 1 -m "ROUTER" -l "error" -s "Command: $cmd, FAILED with status $st from "$0"." 
    return $st 
}
#!/bin/sh

ROUTER_CMD_LOGS=/opt/nvtl/tmp/router2/router2-log

chmod 0664 $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
print_if_failed () 
{ 
    if [ `wc -l $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS | cut -d ' ' -f1` -gt 10000 ]; then 
        echo "$(tail -n 1000 $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS)" > $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS

    $@ 2>> $ROUTER_CMD_LOGS && return  
    st=$? 
    cmd="$*" 
    printf 'Command: "%s" FAILED with status %d from "%s" Please Fix \n' "$*" "$st" "$0" >>$ROUTER_CMD_LOGS 
    nvtl_log -p 1 -m "ROUTER" -l "error" -s "Command: $cmd, FAILED with status $st from "$0"." 
    return $st

#Script to update IPSec Connection Failure Reason.
#Usage- sh <script-name> <tun_idx>
#Return Connection Failure Reason.
#!/bin/sh

LOG="/opt/nvtl/tmp/vpn/ipsec/ipsec_conn$1_log"
state_cmd="establishing IKE_SA failed, peer not responding"
var=`grep -i "$state_cmd" $LOG`
if [ ! -z "$var" ]; then
  echo 7
else
  state_cmd="received NO_PROPOSAL_CHOSEN notify error"
  var=`grep -i "$state_cmd" $LOG`
  if [ ! -z "$var" ]; then
    echo 8
  else
    echo 0
  fi
fi#Script to Change IPSec Default Behavior.
#Usage- sh <script-name>
#!/bin/sh

CHARON_CONFIG="/etc/strongswan.d/charon.conf"
sed -i 's/# retransmit_base/retransmit_base/' $CHARON_CONFIG
sed -i 's/# retransmit_jitter/retransmit_jitter/' $CHARON_CONFIG
sed -i 's/# retransmit_limit = 0/retransmit_limit = 120/' $CHARON_CONFIG
sed -i 's/# retransmit_timeout/retransmit_timeout/' $CHARON_CONFIG
sed -i 's/# retransmit_tries/retransmit_tries/' $CHARON_CONFIG
sed -i 's/# make_before_break = no/make_before_break = yes/' $CHARON_CONFIG
ipsec reload &
#Script to update IPSec current connection status.
#Usage- sh <script-name> <conn-name>
#Return connection status.
#!/bin/sh

state_cmd="ipsec status $1"
var=`eval $state_cmd | grep -i established`
if [[ "$var" == "" ]]; then
var=`eval $state_cmd | grep -vi security | grep CONNECTING`
  if [[ "$var" == "" ]]; then
    #DISCONNECTED
    cur_state=3
    if [ -f /opt/nvtl/data/vpn/ipsec/ipsec_failure_update.sh ]; then
      fail=`sh /opt/nvtl/data/vpn/ipsec/ipsec_failure_update.sh $2`
      if [ $fail -ne "0" ]; then
        #CONNECTION_FAILED
        cur_state=4
      fi
    fi
  else
#Script to update WGuard current connection status.
#Usage- sh <script-name> <conn-name>
#Return connection status.
#!/bin/sh

WGUARD_CMD_LOGS=/opt/nvtl/tmp/vpn/wguard/wgvpn-log
state_cmd="wg show wg$1"
wgshow=$($state_cmd)
date_str=$(date)
printf "${date_str} :\n ${wgshow}
" >> $WGUARD_CMD_LOGS
if [ -z "$wgshow" ] ; then
  date_str=$(date)
  printf "${date_str} :wg$1 - NOT CONNECTED\n" >> $WGUARD_CMD_LOGS
  cur_state=0 #VPN_STATE_NOTCONNECTED
else
  handshake=$($state_cmd | grep "latest handshake:")
  if [ -z "$handshake" ] ; then
    date_str=$(date)
    printf "${date_str} : wg$1: Running, still not connected. Please check network connectivity.\n" >> $WGUARD_CMD_LOGS
#!/bin/sh

### üí¨ Copilot

Summary of findings:
- Shell scripts (e.g., deinit_internet_block.sh, init_internet_block.sh) handle router command logging and VPN/IPSec state management.
- CGI executables (getGPSStatus.cgi, webui.cgi) are present but could not be analyzed with file (likely due to missing tool).
- Shared libraries and app.so modules in /opt/nvtl/lib and /opt/nvtl/webui/apps implement web UI, VPN, SIM, and device control logic, with C++ symbols indicating modular page/component design.
- Most .so and app.so files expose C++ methods for data access, validation, and device/service management.

If you want deeper analysis (e.g., full strings output, or strace on a running process), specify the target file or process.

### üë§ User

### ‚úÖ `bash`

$ mkdir -p FX2000 && adb pull /opt FX2000/opt && for d in bin etc home lib mnt nvtl proc root run sbin srv tmp usr var; do adb pull /$d FX2000/$d; done

2. 
3. #
4. # init.d script for the bckrst listed
5. #
6. BCKRST=bckrstd
7. 
8. export SHELL=/bin/sh PATH=$PATH:/opt/nvtl/bin LD_LIBRARY_PATH=$LD_LIBRARY_PATH:/opt/nvtl/lib
9. 
10. case $1 in
11. 	start)
12. 		echo -n "Starting $BCKRST: "
13. 		start-stop-daemon -S -b -a /opt/nvtl/bin/$BCKRST
14. 		echo "done"
15. 		;;
16. 	stop)
17. 		echo -n "Stopping $BCKRST: "
18. 		start-stop-daemon -K -x /opt/nvtl/bin/$BCKRST
19. 		echo "done"
20. 		;;
21. 	restart)
22. 		$0 stop
23. 		$0 start
24. 		;;
25. 	*)
26. 		echo "Usage: $BCKRST { start | stop | restart}" >&2
27. 		exit 1
28. 		;;
29. esac
```

</details>


---

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 42m 41s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Search for sensitive strings, list ELF files in /opt, and collect executables**

$ grep -R --binary-files=text -I -n -E "-----BEGIN (RSA |ENCRYPTED |)PRIVATE KEY-----|PRIVATE KEY|BEGIN RSA|devagent_client|devagent|private.pem|private_key|devagent_client.key|\.pem$|\.key$|password|passwd|token|secret|api[_-]?key|auth[_-]?token|pkey" FX2000 2>/dev/null | head -n 2000 > FX2000/FX2000_secrets.txt && find FX2000/opt -type f -exec file {} \; | grep ELF > FX2000/FX2000_opt_elfs.txt || true && wc -l FX2000/FX2000_opt_elfs.txt > FX2000/FX2000_opt_elfs_count.txt || true && find FX2000/opt -type f -executable > FX2000/FX2000_executables_list.txt || true && echo done > FX2000/FX2000_scan_done.txt

```

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 45m 9s</sub>

### üí¨ Copilot

Running a full static extraction (strings, file, readelf), packaging ELF files for Ghidra, and scanning /opt, /etc, /var for secrets and executables; will check for qemu for possible emulation.

---

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 45m 58s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Deep static extraction and packaging for Ghidra and secrets scan**

$ set -euo pipefail
mkdir -p FX2000/analysis/strings FX2000/analysis/readelf FX2000/analysis/ghidra
# collect file list
find FX2000/opt FX2000/etc FX2000/var -type f 2>/dev/null > FX2000/analysis/filelist.txt || true
# classify files
find FX2000/opt FX2000/etc FX2000/var -type f -exec file {} \; 2>/dev/null > FX2000/analysis/file_types.txt || true
# extract printable strings for each file (safe fallback if large)
while IFS= read -r f; do out="FX2000/analysis/strings/$(echo "$f" | sed 's#/#_#g')"; mkdir -p "$(dirname "$out")"; strings -a -n 8 "$f" > "$out" 2>/dev/null || true; done < FX2000/analysis/filelist.txt || true
# find ELF files and run readelf
grep -I "ELF" FX2000/analysis/file_types.txt | awk -F: '{print $1}' > FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt || true
if [ -s FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt ]; then
  while IFS= read -r elf; do echo "==== $elf ====" >> FX2000/analysis/readelf_all.txt; readelf -a "$elf" >> FX2000/analysis/readelf_all.txt 2>/dev/null || true; done < FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt || true
fi
# prepare a tarball of /opt ELF and executables for Ghidra import
find FX2000/opt -type f -print0 2>/dev/null | tar --null -cvf FX2000/analysis/for_ghidra.tar --files-from - 2>/dev/null || true
# list executables
find FX2000/opt FX2000/etc FX2000/var -type f -perm /111 -print 2>/dev/null > FX2000/analysis/executables.txt || true
# secrets scan (keys, pem, passwords, tokens)
grep -R --binary-files=text -I -n -E "-----BEGIN |PRIVATE KEY|BEGIN RSA|devagent_client|private.pem|private_key|\.pem$|\.key$|password|passwd|token|secret|api[_-]?key|auth[_-]?token|pkey|devagent|devagent_client.key|devagent_client.pem" FX2000/opt FX2000/etc FX2000/var 2>/dev/null > FX2000/analysis/secrets_full.txt || true
# qemu presence check
command -v qemu-arm-static >/dev/null 2>&1 && echo "qemu-arm-static: yes" > FX2000/analysis/qemu_check.txt || echo "qemu-arm-static: no" > FX2000/analysis/qemu_check.txt
# summarize counts
wc -l FX2000/analysis/filelist.txt FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt FX2000/analysis/executables.txt FX2000/analysis/secrets_full.txt > FX2000/analysis/counts.txt || true
# package analysis outputs
tar -czf FX2000/analysis_results.tar.gz -C FX2000 analysis || true
# mark done
echo done > FX2000/analysis/DONE.txt

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 51m 27s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Run Ghidra analyzeHeadless to import files into a project**

$ set -euo pipefail
GHIDRA_BIN=/snap/bin/ghidra.analyzeHeadless
PROJECT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project"
PROJECT_NAME="FX2000_project"
INPUT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_input"
LOG=FX2000/analysis/ghidra_headless.log
mkdir -p "$PROJECT_DIR"
if [ ! -x "$GHIDRA_BIN" ]; then echo "ERROR: $GHIDRA_BIN not found or not executable" > "$LOG"; exit 1; fi
echo "Starting Ghidra headless import at $(date)" > "$LOG"
# run headless import; keep running even if some files fail
"$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -overwrite -import "$INPUT_DIR" > "$LOG" 2>&1 || true
echo "Ghidra headless finished at $(date)" >> "$LOG"
# produce a brief summary
ls -l "$PROJECT_DIR" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true


```

<exited with exit code 0>
```

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 54m 16s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Run analyzeHeadless per file (capped at 500) to import into Ghidra project**

$ set -euo pipefail
GHIDRA_BIN=/snap/bin/ghidra.analyzeHeadless
PROJECT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project"
PROJECT_NAME="FX2000_project"
INPUT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_input"
LOG=FX2000/analysis/ghidra_headless2.log
mkdir -p "$PROJECT_DIR"
if [ ! -x "$GHIDRA_BIN" ]; then echo "ERROR: $GHIDRA_BIN not found or not executable" > "$LOG"; exit 1; fi

echo "Starting per-file Ghidra headless imports at $(date)" > "$LOG"
first=1
count=0
while IFS= read -r -d '' f; do
  echo "Importing: $f" >> "$LOG"
  if [ "$first" -eq 1 ]; then
    "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -overwrite -import "$f" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true
    first=0
  else
    "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -import "$f" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true
  fi
  count=$((count+1))
  # limit to first 500 files to avoid runaway time; import more on request
  if [ "$count" -ge 500 ]; then
    echo "Reached 500 imports, stopping loop to avoid excessive runtime" >> "$LOG"
    break
  fi
done < <(find "$INPUT_DIR" -type f -print0)

echo "Imported $count files (capped at 500)" >> "$LOG"
echo "Ghidra headless per-file import finished at $(date)" >> "$LOG"
ls -l "$PROJECT_DIR" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true


```
<command with sessionId: 4 is still running after 120 seconds. The command is still running but hasn't produced output yet. It may be waiting for input or still processing. Use read_bash to continue waiting, write_bash if you think it needs input, or stop_bash to stop it.>
```

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 63m 45s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Import ELF binaries from elf_list.txt into Ghidra project (cap 200)**

$ set -euo pipefail
GHIDRA_BIN=/snap/bin/ghidra.analyzeHeadless
PROJECT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project"
PROJECT_NAME="FX2000_project"
ELF_LIST="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt"
LOG=FX2000/analysis/ghidra_elf_import.log
mkdir -p "$PROJECT_DIR"
if [ ! -x "$GHIDRA_BIN" ]; then echo "ERROR: $GHIDRA_BIN not found or not executable" > "$LOG"; exit 1; fi
if [ ! -s "$ELF_LIST" ]; then echo "No ELF list found at $ELF_LIST" > "$LOG"; exit 1; fi

echo "Starting ELF imports at $(date)" > "$LOG"
first=1
count=0
while IFS= read -r elf; do
  [ -z "$elf" ] && continue
  echo "Importing ELF: $elf" >> "$LOG"
  if [ "$first" -eq 1 ]; then
    "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -overwrite -import "$elf" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true
    first=0
  else
    "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -import "$elf" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true
  fi
  count=$((count+1))
  if [ "$count" -ge 200 ]; then
    echo "Reached 200 ELF imports, stopping to avoid long run" >> "$LOG"
    break
  fi
done < "$ELF_LIST"

echo "Imported $count ELF files (capped at 200)" >> "$LOG"
echo "ELF import finished at $(date)" >> "$LOG"
ls -l "$PROJECT_DIR" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true


```
<command with sessionId: 5 is still running after 120 seconds. The command is still running but hasn't produced output yet. It may be waiting for input or still processing. Use read_bash to continue waiting, write_bash if you think it needs input, or stop_bash to stop it.>
```

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 70m 41s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Summarize Ghidra project repository and write summary file**

$ set -euo pipefail
PROJECT_REP="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project/FX2000_project.rep"
SUMMARY="FX2000/analysis/ghidra_import_summary.txt"
{ echo "Ghidra project rep: $PROJECT_REP"; if [ -d "$PROJECT_REP" ]; then echo "Exists: yes"; else echo "Exists: no"; fi; echo; echo "Top-level listing:"; ls -la "$(dirname "$PROJECT_REP")" || true; echo; echo "Count of files in project rep:"; find "$PROJECT_REP" -type f | wc -l; echo; echo "Sample program entries (first 200):"; find "$PROJECT_REP" -type f | sed -n '1,200p'; } > "$SUMMARY"

# also produce a list of imported program names from the project manifest if any
# Ghidra project manifest is FX2000_project.gpr in same dir
GPR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project/FX2000_project.gpr"
if [ -f "$GPR" ]; then echo; echo "Project file ($GPR) contents:" >> "$SUMMARY"; sed -n '1,200p' "$GPR" >> "$SUMMARY"; fi

echo "Summary written to $SUMMARY"


```
Summary written to FX2000/analysis/ghidra_import_summary.txt
<exited with exit code 0>
```

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Run analyzeHeadless -process per ELF to execute export_analysis.py and collect outputs (capped at 200)**

$ set -euo pipefail
GHIDRA_BIN=/snap/bin/ghidra.analyzeHeadless
PROJECT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project"
PROJECT_NAME="FX2000_project"
ELF_LIST="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt"
SCRIPT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_scripts"
LOG=FX2000/analysis/ghidra_elf_exports.log
mkdir -p FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports
: > "$LOG"
count=0
while IFS= read -r elf; do
  [ -z "$elf" ] && continue
  count=$((count+1))
  if [ "$count" -gt 200 ]; then
    echo "Reached 200 files, stopping" >> "$LOG"
    break
  fi
  echo "Processing ($count): $elf" >> "$LOG"
  "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -process "$elf" -postScript export_analysis.py -scriptPath "$SCRIPT_DIR" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || echo "failed: $elf" >> "$LOG"
done < "$ELF_LIST"

echo "Per-file Ghidra script run complete. Processed $count files." >> "$LOG"
ls -l FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true


```

<exited with exit code 0>
```

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 94m 41s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Run analyzeHeadless -process per ELF with absolute paths to execute export_analysis.py**

$ set -euo pipefail
GHIDRA_BIN=/snap/bin/ghidra.analyzeHeadless
PROJECT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project"
PROJECT_NAME="FX2000_project"
ELF_LIST="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt"
SCRIPT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_scripts"
LOG=FX2000/analysis/ghidra_elf_exports2.log
mkdir -p FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports
: > "$LOG"
count=0
while IFS= read -r elf; do
  [ -z "$elf" ] && continue
  # make absolute
  if [[ "$elf" != /* ]]; then
    elf="$(pwd)/$elf"
  fi
  count=$((count+1))
  if [ "$count" -gt 200 ]; then
    echo "Reached 200 files, stopping" >> "$LOG"
    break
  fi
  echo "Processing ($count): $elf" >> "$LOG"
  "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -process "$elf" -postScript export_analysis.py -scriptPath "$SCRIPT_DIR" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || echo "failed: $elf" >> "$LOG"
done < "$ELF_LIST"

echo "Per-file Ghidra script run complete. Processed $count files." >> "$LOG"
ls -l FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true


```

<exited with exit code 0>
```

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 105m 43s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Use analyzeHeadless -import per ELF and run export_analysis.py to extract data**

$ set -euo pipefail
GHIDRA_BIN=/snap/bin/ghidra.analyzeHeadless
PROJECT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_project"
PROJECT_NAME="FX2000_project"
ELF_LIST="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/elf_list.txt"
SCRIPT_DIR="$(pwd)/FX2000/analysis/ghidra_scripts"
LOG=FX2000/analysis/ghidra_elf_import_and_export.log
mkdir -p FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports
: > "$LOG"
count=0
first=1
while IFS= read -r elf; do
  [ -z "$elf" ] && continue
  # make absolute
  if [[ "$elf" != /* ]]; then
    elf="$(pwd)/$elf"
  fi
  count=$((count+1))
  if [ "$count" -gt 200 ]; then
    echo "Reached 200 files, stopping" >> "$LOG"
    break
  fi
  echo "Importing & processing ($count): $elf" >> "$LOG"
  if [ "$first" -eq 1 ]; then
    "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -overwrite -import "$elf" -postScript export_analysis.py -scriptPath "$SCRIPT_DIR" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || echo "failed: $elf" >> "$LOG"
    first=0
  else
    "$GHIDRA_BIN" "$PROJECT_DIR" "$PROJECT_NAME" -import "$elf" -postScript export_analysis.py -scriptPath "$SCRIPT_DIR" >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || echo "failed: $elf" >> "$LOG"
  fi
done < "$ELF_LIST"

echo "Import+export run complete. Processed $count files." >> "$LOG"
ls -l FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports >> "$LOG" 2>&1 || true


```
<command with sessionId: 12 is still running after 600 seconds. The command is still running but hasn't produced output yet. It may be waiting for input or still processing. Use read_bash to continue waiting, write_bash if you think it needs input, or stop_bash to stop it.>
```

<summary>104 lines</summary>

```
1. FX2000 Forensic Extraction & Analysis Report
2. Generated: 2025-12-12T06:40:04Z
3. 
4. Summary
5. -------
6. This report documents the static analysis performed on files pulled from the FX2000 device into the local FX2000/ directory. Focus is on /opt (FX2000/opt/...), with additional system files also collected. The raw extraction files produced during collection and analysis are stored under FX2000/ (e.g., FX2000_bin_strings.txt, FX2000_lib_strings.txt, FX2000_secrets.txt, FX2000_opt_elfs.txt, FX2000_executables_list.txt).
7. 
8. Methodology
9. -----------
10. - Pulled /opt and common root directories from the device (excluding /sys and /dev).
11. - Used file(1) to classify binaries and libraries.
12. - Extracted printable strings (strings(1)) for bins and libs and saved them under FX2000/.
13. - Attempted dynamic tracing (strace) locally but failed because the downloaded binaries are ARM 32-bit ELF objects and cannot be executed on this x86 host (Exec format error). Dynamic analysis must be run on an ARM host or under qemu-user/qemu-user-static emulation with correct interpreter.
14. - Performed lightweight disassembly attempts using capstone in a Python venv for initial inspection (capstone installed in fx2000venv). For thorough static reverse engineering, import ELF files into Ghidra or IDA Pro on a host with sufficient memory and CPU.
15. - Searched recursively for sensitive strings and keys and saved matches to FX2000/FX2000_secrets.txt.
16. 
17. Key Findings (high level)
18. -------------------------
19. - The /opt/nvtl tree contains a full web UI, daemons, and many libraries.
20. - Many ELF binaries in /opt/nvtl/bin are 32-bit ARM (ELF 32-bit LSB, ARM, EABI5). Some are stripped; others include debug_info.
21. - The web UI uses many app.so modules (C++ symbols present) under /opt/nvtl/webui/apps/*.so implementing page components and device logic.
22. - There are multiple cryptographic artifacts and certificates present in /opt/nvtl/etc and /opt/nvtl/bin and /opt/nvtl/data (see Sensitive Findings below).
23. 
24. Sensitive Findings (high priority)
25. ---------------------------------
26. See FX2000/FX2000_secrets.txt for raw matches. Highlights found by the automated grep include (paths are relative to the repo root):
27. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/bin/private.pem  (private key file present)
28. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/bin/public.pem   (public key)
29. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/deviceagent/devagent_client.key  (device agent client private key)
30. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/deviceagent/devagent_client.pem  (device agent client certificate)
31. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/etc/fota/device.pem  (FOTA certificate)
32. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/etc/nua/* (device certs and keys present)
33. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/sms/sms_inbox.db and FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/sms/sms_history.txt (user SMS data)
34. - Numerous .pem and .key files under FX2000/opt/nvtl/etc and FX2000/opt/nvtl/data (see FX2000_secrets.txt for full list)
35. 
36. These files contain cryptographic material or personally identifiable communications (SMS DB) and should be treated as sensitive.
37. 
38. Binary & Library Characteristics
39. --------------------------------
40. - All examined ELF binaries under FX2000/opt are ARM 32-bit (ELF 32-bit LSB, ARM, EABI5). Example binaries: ans_cli, ansd, atcmd, bckrst_cli, bckrstd, cloud_engined, deviceagentd, modem2d, vpnd, many others.
41. - Many libraries exist under FX2000/opt/nvtl/lib, e.g., libmodem2_api.so, libmifi_config.so, libmifi_debug.so, libmifimsgbus.so, libs such as libxmldata_api.so. Several libs provide device-specific APIs (sim_mgr, gps, wifi, vpn, fota, etc.).
42. - C++ symbols in webui app.so modules indicate a modular page/component architecture; these modules interface with the web front-end (lighttpd/fastcgi).
43. 
44. Limitations and Notes
45. ---------------------
46. - Dynamic tracing (strace) and running binaries was attempted locally but fails on x86 host due to architecture mismatch (ARM ELFs). To run these binaries and capture runtime behavior and syscalls, use an ARM environment or qemu-user-static with proper loader (/lib/ld-linux-armhf.so.3) and chroot /proc mount as needed.
47. - Capstone was installed in a Python virtual environment for small disassembly attempts, but a thorough static reverse-engineering workflow should use Ghidra/IDA with ARM processor module and run full function analysis. Ghidra will allow decompilation and easier cross-references.
48. 
49. Artifacts produced (local)
50. -------------------------
51. - FX2000/FX2000_bin_strings.txt  -- aggregated strings() output from many /opt/nvtl/bin files
52. - FX2000/FX2000_lib_strings.txt  -- aggregated strings() output from /opt/nvtl/lib/*.so
53. - FX2000/FX2000_cgi_strings.txt  -- strings from CGI executables (webui.cgi, getGPSStatus.cgi)
54. - FX2000/FX2000_secrets.txt      -- grep results for likely sensitive strings (keys, certs, tokens, password occurrences)
55. - FX2000/FX2000_opt_elfs.txt     -- list of ELF files detected under FX2000/opt with file(1) output
56. - FX2000/FX2000_opt_elfs_count.txt -- number of ELF files reported (one-line count file)
57. - FX2000/FX2000_executables_list.txt -- list of executable files under FX2000/opt
58. - FX2000/FX2000_analysis_sample.txt -- sample output summary produced during analysis
59. 
60. Recommended next steps (for deeper analysis)
61. --------------------------------------------
62. 1. Sensitive material handling
63.    - Immediately restrict access to the extracted FX2000/ directory; move to an encrypted analysis host if not already.
64.    - If extracting secret values (private keys) for legitimate recovery, ensure they are stored in secure vaults and rotated on device fleet if keys were exposed.
65. 
66. 2. Dynamic analysis
67.    - Set up an ARM-compatible environment (ARM VM or qemu-user-static). Example quick approach:
68.      - On a Linux host: apt-get install qemu-user-static; copy /usr/bin/qemu-arm-static into chroot, bind-mount /proc and /sys; run binaries and capture strace output.
69.    - Run key daemons (carefully, in isolated environment) and use strace/ltrace to capture syscall behavior.
70. 
71. 3. Static reverse engineering
72.    - Import selected ELF(s) (e.g., libmodem2_api.so, modem2d, webui app.so modules) into Ghidra and run analysis. Focus on functions that handle SMS, NV item access, QMI, and authentication.
73.    - Use the not-stripped libraries (those with debug_info) first ‚Äî they provide symbol names and types that speed up understanding.
74. 
75. 4. Secrets enumeration
76.    - Review FX2000/FX2000_secrets.txt and manually inspect any matched files. Look for literal private keys (-----BEGIN ... PRIVATE KEY-----), credentials in config.xml files, or hard-coded tokens.
77. 
78. 5. Export actionable indicators
79.    - Produce a redaction-safe list of command names, interfaces, and library calls related to modem access (QMI libraries, diag, AT interpretors) to integrate into testing/automation tools.
80. 
81. Important file locations to inspect immediately
82. ---------------------------------------------
83. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/bin/private.pem
84. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/bin/public.pem
85. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/deviceagent/devagent_client.key
86. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/deviceagent/devagent_client.pem
87. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/etc/fota/device.pem
88. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/sms/sms_inbox.db
89. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/data/sms/sms_history.txt
90. - FX2000/opt/nvtl/lib/*.so  (esp. libmodem2_api.so, libmal_qct.so, libqmi*.so)
91. 
92. Where to find raw outputs
93. ------------------------
94. - FX2000/FX2000_secrets.txt
95. - FX2000/FX2000_bin_strings.txt
96. - FX2000/FX2000_lib_strings.txt
97. - FX2000/FX2000_opt_elfs.txt
98. - FX2000/FX2000_executables_list.txt
99. 
100. Concluding notes
101. ----------------
102. This report is an initial static reconnaissance and sensitive-data enumeration. It intentionally does not modify any collected files. For full protocol- and logic-level understanding, perform deeper static RE in Ghidra and dynamic tracing in an ARM-compatible sandbox. If desired, next actions can include: (A) produce a prioritized list of binaries to reverse (by connectivity/privilege), (B) extract and redact private keys and provide a secure backup to the owner, (C) create automated scripts to run the most important tools under qemu and capture runtime behavior.
103. 
104. End of report.
```

</details>

<sub>‚è±Ô∏è 119m 14s</sub>

### ‚úÖ `bash`

**Append Ghidra summary to FX2000.md**

$ set -euo pipefail
COUNT=$(ls -1 FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports | wc -l || true)
SAMPLE=$(ls -1 FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports | sed -n '1,10p' || true)
cat >> FX2000.md <<EOF

Ghidra Headless Export Summary
-----------------------------
- Export directory: FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports
- Number of exported files: ${COUNT}
- Sample exported files (first 10):
${SAMPLE}

Notes:
- For each imported ELF, Ghidra produced <program>_functions.csv, <program>_strings.csv and <program>_summary.md where present.
- Use the CSVs for automated parsing of functions and string->function cross references.
- Sensitive strings previously identified are also present in these exports; review FX2000/analysis/ghidra_exports/*_strings.csv for context and references.

End of report.
EOF

echo "FX2000.md updated with Ghidra export summary"


```
FX2000.md updated with Ghidra export summary
<exited with exit code 0>
```
